<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Union territory of India</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"National Capital Territory" redirects here. For the generic term, see <a href="/wiki/Capital_districts_and_territories" title="Capital districts and territories">Capital districts and territories</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>, the capital of India, entirely within the limits of Delhi.</div>

href="#cite_note-pc-census2011-10">[10]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Delhi</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[a]</a></sup> officially the <b>National Capital Territory</b> (<b>NCT</b>) <b>of Delhi</b>, is a city and a <a href="/wiki/Union_territory" title="Union territory">union territory</a> of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> containing <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>, the capital of India. Straddling the <a href="/wiki/Yamuna" title="Yamuna">Yamuna</a> river, primarily its western or right bank, Delhi shares borders with the state of <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a> in the east and with the state of <a href="/wiki/Haryana" title="Haryana">Haryana</a> in the remaining directions. The NCT covers an area of 1,484 square kilometres (573 sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delhi_Info_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Delhi_Info-5">[5]</a></sup> According to the 2011 census, Delhi's city proper population was over 11 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_census_Delhi_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-2011_census_Delhi-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> while the NCT's population was about 16.8 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-delhi2011_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-delhi2011-7">[7]</a></sup> Delhi's urban agglomeration, which includes the satellite cities of <a href="/wiki/Ghaziabad" title="Ghaziabad">Ghaziabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faridabad" title="Faridabad">Faridabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gurgaon" title="Gurgaon">Gurgaon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Noida" title="Noida">Noida</a> in an area known as the <a href="/wiki/National_Capital_Region_(India)" title="National Capital Region (India)">National Capital Region</a> (NCR), has an estimated population of over 28 million, making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_areas_in_India" title="List of metropolitan areas in India">largest metropolitan area in India</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_urban_areas_by_population" title="List of urban areas by population">second-largest in the world</a> (after <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNcities2018_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNcities2018-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The topography of the medieval fort <a href="/wiki/Purana_Qila" title="Purana Qila">Purana Qila</a> on the banks of the river Yamuna matches the literary description of the citadel <a href="/wiki/Indraprastha" title="Indraprastha">Indraprastha</a> in the Sanskrit epic <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i>; however, excavations in the area have revealed no signs of an ancient built environment. From the early 13th century until the mid-19th century, Delhi was the capital of two major empires, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delhi_sultanate" title="Delhi sultanate">Delhi sultanate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>, which covered large parts of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>. All three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Sites</a> in the city, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qutub_Minar" title="Qutub Minar">Qutub Minar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humayun%27s_Tomb" title="Humayun's Tomb">Humayun's Tomb</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Fort" title="Red Fort">Red Fort</a>, belong to this period. Delhi was the early centre of <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qawwali" title="Qawwali">Qawwali</a> music.  The names of <a href="/wiki/Nizamuddin_Auliya" title="Nizamuddin Auliya">Nizamuddin Auliya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amir_Khusrau" title="Amir Khusrau">Amir Khusrau</a> are prominently associated with it. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khariboli" title="Khariboli">Khariboli</a> dialect of Delhi was part of a linguistic development that gave rise to the literature of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urdu_language" title="Urdu language">Urdu language</a> and then of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Hindi" title="Modern Standard Hindi">Modern Standard Hindi</a>. Major Urdu poets from Delhi include <a href="/wiki/Mir_Taqi_Mir" title="Mir Taqi Mir">Mir Taqi Mir</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mirza_Ghalib" title="Mirza Ghalib">Mirza Ghalib</a>. Delhi was a major centre of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">Indian Rebellion of 1857</a>. In 1911, <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>, a southern region within Delhi, became the capital of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Indian_Empire" title="British Indian Empire">British Indian Empire</a>.  During the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_India" title="Partition of India">Partition of India</a> in 1947, Delhi was transformed from a Mughal city to a Punjabi one, losing two-thirds of its Muslim residents, in part due to the pressure brought to bear by arriving Hindu refugees from western Punjab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-talbot-singh-delhi_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-talbot-singh-delhi-16">[15]</a></sup> After independence in 1947, New Delhi continued as the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_India" title="Dominion of India">Dominion of India</a>, and after 1950 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_India" title="Republic of India">Republic of India</a>.  
</p><p>Delhi ranks <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Indian_states_and_territories_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of Indian states and territories by Human Development Index">fifth among the Indian states and union territories</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_development_index" title="Human development index">human development index</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snhdi-gdl_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-snhdi-gdl-17">[16]</a></sup> Delhi has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Indian_states_and_union_territories_by_GDP_per_capita" title="List of Indian states and union territories by GDP per capita">second-highest</a> <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP per capita</a> in India (after <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goa</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delhi_Budget_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Delhi_Budget-18">[17]</a></sup> Although a <a href="/wiki/Union_territory" title="Union territory">union territory</a>, the political administration of the NCT of Delhi today more closely resembles that of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/States_of_India" title="States of India">state</a> of India, with its own legislature, high court and an executive council of ministers headed by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chief_Minister_of_Delhi" title="Chief Minister of Delhi">Chief Minister</a>. New Delhi is jointly administered by the federal <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India" title="Government of India">government of India</a> and the local <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Delhi" title="Government of Delhi">government of Delhi</a>, and serves as the capital of the nation as well as the NCT of Delhi. Delhi is also the centre of the National Capital Region, which is an "interstate regional planning" area created in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-profile_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-profile-20">[19]</a></sup>
Delhi hosted the inaugural <a href="/wiki/1951_Asian_Games" title="1951 Asian Games">1951 Asian Games</a>, the <a href="/wiki/1982_Asian_Games" title="1982 Asian Games">1982 Asian Games</a>, the 1983 <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a> summit, the <a href="/wiki/2010_Men%27s_Hockey_World_Cup" title="2010 Men's Hockey World Cup">2010 Men's Hockey World Cup</a>, the <a href="/wiki/2010_Commonwealth_Games" title="2010 Commonwealth Games">2010 Commonwealth Games</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/4th_BRICS_summit" title="4th BRICS summit">2012 BRICS summit</a> and was one of the major host cities of the <a href="/wiki/2011_Cricket_World_Cup" title="2011 Cricket World Cup">2011 Cricket World Cup</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toponym">Toponym</span></h2>
<p>There are a number of myths and legends associated with the origin of the name <i>Delhi</i>. One of them is derived from <i>Dhillu</i> or <i>Dilu</i>, a king who built a city at this location in 50 BCE and named it after himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecosurv1_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-ecosurv1-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dhillu_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-dhillu-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geobritish_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-geobritish-23">[22]</a></sup> Another legend holds that the name of the city is based on the <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>/<a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrit</a> word <span title="Hindi-language romanization"><i lang="hi-Latn">dhili</i></span> (<i>loose</i>) and that it was used by the <a href="/wiki/Tomara_dynasty" title="Tomara dynasty">Tomaras</a> to refer to the city because the <a href="/wiki/Iron_pillar_of_Delhi" title="Iron pillar of Delhi">iron pillar of Delhi</a> had a weak foundation and had to be moved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geobritish_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-geobritish-23">[22]</a></sup> According to Panjab Notes and Queries, the name of the city at the time of King Prithviraj was <span title="Hindi-language romanization"><i lang="hi-Latn">dilpat</i></span>, and that <span title="Hindi-language romanization"><i lang="hi-Latn">dilpat</i></span> and <span title="Hindi-language romanization"><i lang="hi-Latn">dilli</i></span> are probably derived from the old Hindi word <span title="Hindi-language romanization"><i lang="hi-Latn">dil</i></span> meaning "eminence". The former director of the Archaeological Survey of India, Alexander Cunningham, mentioned that <span title="Hindi-language romanization"><i lang="hi-Latn">dilli</i></span> later became <span title="Hindi-language romanization"><i lang="hi-Latn">dihli/dehli</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> Some suggest the coins in circulation in the region under the Tomaras were called <i>dehliwal</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncertVII_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncertVII-25">[24]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Bhavishya_Purana" title="Bhavishya Purana">Bhavishya Purana</a>, King Prithiviraja of <a href="/wiki/Indraprastha" title="Indraprastha">Indraprastha</a> built a new fort in the modern-day <a href="/wiki/Purana_Qila" title="Purana Qila">Purana Qila</a> area for the convenience of all four castes in his kingdom. He ordered the construction of a gateway to the fort and later named the fort <i>dehali</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historyhistoryabtindra_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-historyhistoryabtindra-26">[25]</a></sup> Some historians believe that <i>Dhilli</i> or <i>Dhillika</i> is the original name for the city while others believe the name could be a corruption of the <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani</a> words <i>dehleez</i> or <i>dehali</i>—both terms meaning "threshold" or "gateway"—and symbolic of the city as a gateway to the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Gangetic_Plain" title="Indo-Gangetic Plain">Gangetic Plain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cohen_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-cohen-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dhilika_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-dhilika-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The people of Delhi are referred to as <i>Delhiites</i> or <i>Dilliwalas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> The city is referenced in various idioms of the Northern <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan languages</a>. Examples include:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Abhī Dillī dūr hai</i> (अभी दिल्ली दूर है / ابھی دلی دور ہے) or its <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> version, <i>Hanuz Dehli dur ast</i> (<span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">هنوز دهلی دور است</span></span>), literally meaning "Delhi is still far away", which is generically said about a task or journey still far from completion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref46cojam_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref46cojam-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref88difob_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref88difob-32">[31]</a></sup></li>
<li><span title="Hindi-language romanization"><i lang="hi-Latn">Ās-pās barse, Dillī pānī tarse</i></span> (आस-पास बरसे, दिल्ली पानी तरसे \ آس پاس برسے، دلی پانی ترسے), literally meaning "It pours all around, while Delhi lies parched". An allusion to the sometimes semi-arid climate of Delhi, it idiomatically refers to situations of deprivation when one is surrounded by plenty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref88difob_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-ref88difob-32">[31]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The form <i>Delhi</i>, used in <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> and strangely with an <i>h</i> following an <i>l</i>, originated under colonial rule and is a corrupt spelling based on the Urdu name of the city (<span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">دہلی</span>, <i>Dehli</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Delhi" title="History of Delhi">History of Delhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Delhi" title="Old Delhi">Old Delhi</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_and_Early_Medieval_Periods">Ancient and Early Medieval Periods</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Purana_Qila_ramparts,_Delhi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Purana_Qila_ramparts%2C_Delhi.jpg/220px-Purana_Qila_ramparts%2C_Delhi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Purana_Qila_ramparts%2C_Delhi.jpg/330px-Purana_Qila_ramparts%2C_Delhi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Purana_Qila_ramparts%2C_Delhi.jpg/440px-Purana_Qila_ramparts%2C_Delhi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Purana_Qila_ramparts,_Delhi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The walls of the 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Purana_Qila" title="Purana Qila">Purana Qila</a> built on a mound matching ancient literary descriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asher-indraprastha_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-asher-indraprastha-34">[33]</a></sup></div></div></div><p> Traditionally seven cities have been associated with the region of Delhi.  The earliest, <a href="/wiki/Indraprastha" title="Indraprastha">Indraprastha</a>, is part of a literary description in the Sanskrit epic <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i> (composed c. 400 BCE to 200 CE but describing an earlier time) which situates a city on a knoll on the banks of the river Yamuna.  According to art historian Catherine B. Asher, the topographical description of the <i>Mahabharata</i> matches the area of <a href="/wiki/Purana_Qila" title="Purana Qila">Purana Qila</a>, a 14th-century CE fort of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delhi_sultanate" title="Delhi sultanate">Delhi sultanate</a>, but the analogy does not go much further. Whereas the <i>Mahabharata</i> speaks of a beautifully decorated city with surrounding fortification, the excavations have yielded "uneven findings of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Painted_Grey_Ware" title="Painted Grey Ware">painted grey pottery</a> characteristic of the eleventh century BCE; no signs of a built environment, much fewer fortifications, have been revealed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asher-indraprastha_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-asher-indraprastha-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest architectural relics date back to the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya</a> period (c. 300 BCE); in 1966, an inscription of the Mauryan Emperor <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a> (273–235 BCE) was discovered near Srinivaspuri. Remains of several major cities can be found in Delhi. The first of these was in the southern part of present-day Delhi. King <a href="/wiki/Anangpal_Tomar" title="Anangpal Tomar">Anang Pal</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tomara_dynasty" title="Tomara dynasty">Tomara dynasty</a> built <a href="/wiki/Qila_Rai_Pithora" title="Qila Rai Pithora">Lal Kot</a> and several temples in 1052 CE. <a href="/wiki/Vigraharaja_IV" title="Vigraharaja IV">Vigraharaj Chauhan</a> conquered Lal Kot in the mid-12th century and renamed it <a href="/wiki/Qila_Rai_Pithora" title="Qila Rai Pithora">Qila Rai Pithora</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Medieval_Period">Late Medieval Period</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qutub_-_Minar,_Delhi_(6994969674).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4288" data-file-width="2848" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Qutub_-_Minar%2C_Delhi_%286994969674%29.jpg/170px-Qutub_-_Minar%2C_Delhi_%286994969674%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Qutub_-_Minar%2C_Delhi_%286994969674%29.jpg/255px-Qutub_-_Minar%2C_Delhi_%286994969674%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Qutub_-_Minar%2C_Delhi_%286994969674%29.jpg/340px-Qutub_-_Minar%2C_Delhi_%286994969674%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qutub_-_Minar,_Delhi_(6994969674).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Qutub Minar, Delhi.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Prithviraj_Chauhan" title="Prithviraj Chauhan">Prithviraj Chauhan</a> was defeated in 1192 by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_of_Ghor" title="Muhammad of Ghor">Muhammad Ghori</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_battle_of_Tarain" title="Second battle of Tarain">second battle of Tarain</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qutb_al-Din_Aibak" title="Qutb al-Din Aibak">Qutb-ud-din Aibak</a>, was given the responsibility of governing the conquered territories of India until Ghori returned to his capital, <a href="/wiki/Ghor_Province" title="Ghor Province">Ghor</a>. When Ghori died without an heir in 1206 CE,  Qutb-ud-din assumed control of Ghori's Indian possessions, and laid the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_dynasty_(Delhi)" title="Mamluk dynasty (Delhi)">Mamluk dynasty</a>. He began construction of the <a href="/wiki/Qutb_Minar" title="Qutb Minar">Qutb Minar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qutb_complex" title="Qutb complex">Quwwat-al-Islam</a> (Might of Islam) mosque, the earliest extant mosque in India. It was his successor, <a href="/wiki/Iltutmish" title="Iltutmish">Iltutmish</a> (1211–1236), who consolidated the Turkic conquest of northern India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecosurv1_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-ecosurv1-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quwwat_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quwwat-35">[34]</a></sup>  At 72.5 m (238 ft), the <a href="/wiki/Qutb_Minar" title="Qutb Minar">Qutb Minar</a>, a <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_India" title="List of World Heritage Sites in India">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a> in Delhi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qutab_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qutab-36">[35]</a></sup> was completed during the reign of Sultan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illtutmish" title="Illtutmish">Illtutmish</a> in the 13th century. Although its style has some similarities with the <a href="/wiki/Jarkurgan_minaret" title="Jarkurgan minaret">Jarkurgan minaret</a>, it is more closely related to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghaznavid" title="Ghaznavid">Ghaznavid</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghurid" title="Ghurid">Ghurid</a> minarets of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcclary-medieval-monuments_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcclary-medieval-monuments-37">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Razia_Sultana" title="Razia Sultana">Razia</a>, daughter of Iltutmish, became the Sultana of Delhi upon the former's death.
</p><p>For the next three hundred years, Delhi was ruled by a succession of <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Afghans" title="Afghans">Afghan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lodi_dynasty" title="Lodi dynasty">Lodi dynasty</a>. They built several forts and townships that are part of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Delhi" title="History of Delhi">seven cities of Delhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> Delhi was a major centre of <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a> during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_dynasty_(Delhi)" title="Mamluk dynasty (Delhi)">Mamluk Sultanate (Delhi)</a> was overthrown in 1290 by <a href="/wiki/Jalal-ud-din_Khalji" title="Jalal-ud-din Khalji">Jalal ud din Firuz Khalji</a> (1290–1320). Under the second Khalji ruler, <a href="/wiki/Alauddin_Khalji" title="Alauddin Khalji">Ala-ud-din Khalji</a>, the Delhi sultanate extended its control south of the <a href="/wiki/Narmada_River" title="Narmada River">Narmada River</a> in the Deccan. The Delhi sultanate reached its greatest extent during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_bin_Tughluq" title="Muhammad bin Tughluq">Muhammad bin Tughluq</a> (1325–1351). In an attempt to bring the whole of the Deccan under control, he moved his capital to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daulatabad,_Maharashtra" title="Daulatabad, Maharashtra">Daulatabad, Maharashtra</a> in central India. However, by moving away from Delhi he lost control of the north and was forced to return to Delhi to restore order. The southern provinces then broke away. In the years following the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firoz_Shah_Tughlaq" title="Firoz Shah Tughlaq">Firoz Shah Tughlaq</a> (1351–1388), the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> rapidly began to lose its hold over its northern provinces. Delhi was captured and sacked by <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> in 1398,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> who massacred 100,000 captive civilian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Delhi's decline continued under the <a href="/wiki/Sayyid_dynasty" title="Sayyid dynasty">Sayyid dynasty</a> (1414–1451), until the sultanate was reduced to Delhi and its hinterland. Under the Afghan <a href="/wiki/Lodi_dynasty" title="Lodi dynasty">Lodi dynasty</a> (1451–1526), the Delhi sultanate recovered control of Punjab and the Gangetic plain to once again achieve domination over Northern India. However, the recovery was short-lived and the sultanate was destroyed in 1526 by <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal dynasty</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Modern_Period">Early Modern Period</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Delhi_Red_fort.jpg"><img alt="Red Fort with the Indian Flag at the centre" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Delhi_Red_fort.jpg/220px-Delhi_Red_fort.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Delhi_Red_fort.jpg/330px-Delhi_Red_fort.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Delhi_Red_fort.jpg/440px-Delhi_Red_fort.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Delhi_Red_fort.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Red_Fort" title="Red Fort">Red Fort</a>, a <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_India" title="List of World Heritage Sites in India">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>, was the main residence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Mughal_emperors" title="List of Mughal emperors">Mughal emperors</a> for nearly 200 years.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1526, <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Descent_from_Genghis_Khan" title="Descent from Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a>, from the Fergana Valley in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> invaded India, defeated the last Lodhi sultan in the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Panipat" title="First Battle of Panipat">First Battle of Panipat</a> and founded the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> that ruled from Delhi and <a href="/wiki/Agra" title="Agra">Agra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecosurv1_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-ecosurv1-21">[20]</a></sup> The Mughal dynasty ruled Delhi for more than three centuries, with a sixteen-year hiatus during the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Sher_Shah_Suri" title="Sher Shah Suri">Sher Shah Suri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hemu" title="Hemu">Hemu</a> from 1540 to 1556.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shershah_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-shershah-42">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a> built the seventh city of Delhi that bears his name <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shahjahanabad" title="Shahjahanabad">Shahjahanabad</a></i>, which served as the capital of the Mughal Empire from 1638 and is today known as the <i>Old City</i> or <i>Old Delhi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book11111_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-book11111-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the death of <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a> in 1707, the Mughal Empire's influence declined rapidly as the Hindu <a href="/wiki/Maratha_Empire" title="Maratha Empire">Maratha Empire</a> from <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Plateau" title="Deccan Plateau">Deccan Plateau</a> rose to prominence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> In 1737, Maratha forces led by <a href="/wiki/Baji_Rao_I" title="Baji Rao I">Baji Rao I</a> sacked Delhi following their victory against the Mughals in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Delhi_(1737)" title="Battle of Delhi (1737)">First Battle of Delhi</a>. In 1739, the Mughal Empire lost the huge <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Karnal" title="Battle of Karnal">Battle of Karnal</a> in less than three hours against the numerically outnumbered but militarily superior Persian army led by <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah" title="Nader Shah">Nader Shah</a> of <a href="/wiki/Afsharid_dynasty" title="Afsharid dynasty">Persia.</a> After his <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah%27s_invasion_of_India" title="Nader Shah's invasion of India">invasion</a>, he <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sack_of_Delhi" title="Sack of Delhi">completely sacked and looted Delhi</a>, carrying away immense wealth including the <a href="/wiki/Peacock_Throne" title="Peacock Throne">Peacock Throne</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Daria-i-Noor" title="Daria-i-Noor">Daria-i-Noor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Koh-i-Noor" title="Koh-i-Noor">Koh-i-Noor</a>. The Mughals, severely further weakened, could never overcome this crushing defeat and humiliation which also left the way open for more invaders to come, including eventually the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah" title="Nader Shah">Nader</a> eventually agreed to leave the city and India after forcing the Mughal emperor <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Shah" title="Muhammad Shah">Muhammad Shah</a> I to beg him for mercy and granting him the keys of the city and the royal treasury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> A treaty signed in 1752 made Marathas the protectors of the Mughal throne in Delhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> The city was sacked again in 1757 by the forces of <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Durrani" title="Ahmad Shah Durrani">Ahmad Shah Durrani</a>, although it was not annexed by the <a href="/wiki/Durrani_Empire" title="Durrani Empire">Afghan Empire</a> and being its vassal state under the Mughal emperor. Then the Marathas battled and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Delhi_(1757)" title="Battle of Delhi (1757)">won</a> control of Delhi from the Mughals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> By the end of the century, Delhi had also come under control of the <a href="/wiki/Bharatpur_State" title="Bharatpur State">Bharatpur State</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Empire" title="Sikh Empire">Sikh Empire</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_Period">Colonial Period</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NewDelhiInaugurationSecondDayCancellation27Feb1931.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1321" data-file-width="2233" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NewDelhiInaugurationSecondDayCancellation27Feb1931.jpg/220px-NewDelhiInaugurationSecondDayCancellation27Feb1931.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NewDelhiInaugurationSecondDayCancellation27Feb1931.jpg/330px-NewDelhiInaugurationSecondDayCancellation27Feb1931.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NewDelhiInaugurationSecondDayCancellation27Feb1931.jpg/440px-NewDelhiInaugurationSecondDayCancellation27Feb1931.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NewDelhiInaugurationSecondDayCancellation27Feb1931.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Presidencies_and_provinces_of_British_India" title="Presidencies and provinces of British India">British India</a> stamps, inauguration, New Delhi, February 1931</div></div></div>
<p>In 1803, during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Anglo-Maratha_War" title="Second Anglo-Maratha War">Second Anglo-Maratha War</a>, the forces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" title="British East India Company">British East India Company</a> defeated the Maratha forces in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Delhi,_1803" title="Battle of Delhi, 1803">Battle of Delhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup>
During the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">Indian Rebellion of 1857</a>, Delhi fell to the forces of East India Company after a bloody fight known as the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Delhi" title="Siege of Delhi">Siege of Delhi</a>. The city came under the direct control of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Government" title="British Government">British Government</a> in 1858. It was made a district province of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_(British_India)" title="Punjab (British India)">Punjab</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecosurv1_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-ecosurv1-21">[20]</a></sup> In 1911, it was announced that the capital of British-held territories in India was to be transferred from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcutta" title="Calcutta">Calcutta</a> to Delhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cal_to_del_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-cal_to_del-52">[51]</a></sup> The name "New Delhi" was given in 1927, and the new capital was inaugurated on 13 February 1931. <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a> was officially declared as the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_India" title="Dominion of India">Union of India</a> after the country gained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republic_of_India" title="History of the Republic of India">independence</a> on 15 August 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>  It has expanded since; the small part of it that was constructed during the British period has come to be informally known as <i>Lutyens' Delhi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lutdelhiii_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-lutdelhiii-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Partition_and_post-independence">Partition and post-independence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Delhi_India_~Khan_Market.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/New_Delhi_India_~Khan_Market.jpg/220px-New_Delhi_India_~Khan_Market.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/New_Delhi_India_~Khan_Market.jpg/330px-New_Delhi_India_~Khan_Market.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/New_Delhi_India_~Khan_Market.jpg/440px-New_Delhi_India_~Khan_Market.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Delhi_India_~Khan_Market.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Khan_Market" title="Khan Market">Khan Market</a> in New Delhi, now a high-end shopping district, was established in 1951 to help refugees of the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_India" title="Partition of India">Partition of India</a>, especially those from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_West_Frontier_Province" title="North West Frontier Province">North West Frontier Province</a> (NWFP).  It honours <a href="/wiki/Khan_Abdul_Jabbar_Khan" title="Khan Abdul Jabbar Khan">Khan Abdul Jabbar Khan</a>, Chief Minister of NWFP during the Partition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lakhani-indexpress-khan_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-lakhani-indexpress-khan-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bhardwaj-khan-reuters_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-bhardwaj-khan-reuters-56">[55]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_India" title="Partition of India">partition of India</a>, around five lakh Hindu and Sikh refugees, mainly from <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Punjab, Pakistan">West Punjab</a> fled to Delhi, while around three lakh Muslim residents of the city migrated to Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Ethnic Punjabis are believed to account for at least 40% of Delhi's total population and are predominantly <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>-speaking <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_Hindus" title="Punjabi Hindus">Punjabi Hindus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanjay2008_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanjay2008-61">[60]</a></sup> Migration to Delhi from the rest of India continues (as of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Delhi&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>), contributing more to the rise of Delhi's population than the birth rate, which is declining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-migrationbirth_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-migrationbirth-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/States_Reorganisation_Act,_1956" title="States Reorganisation Act, 1956">States Reorganisation Act, 1956</a> created the Union Territory of Delhi from its predecessor, the <i><a href="/wiki/Chief_Commissioner%27s_Province" title="Chief Commissioner's Province">Chief Commissioner's Province</a> of Delhi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7thAmend56_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-7thAmend56-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReorgAct56_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReorgAct56-2">[2]</a></sup>
The Constitution (Sixty-ninth Amendment) Act, 1991 declared the Union Territory of Delhi to be formally known as the National Capital Territory of Delhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCTact_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCTact-3">[3]</a></sup> The Act gave Delhi its legislative assembly along Civil lines, though with limited powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCTact_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-NCTact-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Delhi was the primary site in the nationwide <a href="/wiki/1984_anti-Sikh_riots" title="1984 anti-Sikh riots">anti-Sikh pogroms</a> of 1984, which resulted in the death of around 2,800 people in the city according to government figures, though independent estimates of the number of people killed tend to be higher. The riots were set off by the <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Indira_Gandhi" title="Assassination of Indira Gandhi">assassination of Indira Gandhi</a>—the Prime Minister of India at the time—by her Sikh bodyguards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2001, the Parliament of India building in New Delhi was <a href="/wiki/2001_Indian_Parliament_attack" title="2001 Indian Parliament attack">attacked</a> by armed militants, killing six security personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> India suspected Pakistan-based militant groups were behind the attack, which caused a major <a href="/wiki/2001%E2%80%932002_India%E2%80%93Pakistan_standoff" title="2001–2002 India–Pakistan standoff">diplomatic crisis</a> between the two countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> There were further terrorist attacks in Delhi in <a href="/wiki/2005_Delhi_bombings" title="2005 Delhi bombings">2005</a> and <a href="/wiki/13_September_2008_Delhi_bombings" title="13 September 2008 Delhi bombings">2008</a>, resulting in a total of 92 deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news24.com_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-news24.com-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/2020_Delhi_riots" title="2020 Delhi riots">2020 Delhi riots</a>, Delhi's worst communal violence in decades, was caused mainly by Hindu mobs attacking Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian-3-16-20-1_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-3-16-20-1-68">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Analysis-March1_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Analysis-March1-69">[68]</a></sup> Of the 53 people killed, two-thirds were Muslims,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes-2020-3-12-two-thirds_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-2020-3-12-two-thirds-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo-3-6-20-slater-1_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapo-3-6-20-slater-1-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo-3-2-20-slater-1_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapo-3-2-20-slater-1-72">[71]</a></sup> and the rest <a href="/wiki/Hindus" title="Hindus">Hindus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo-3-6-20-slater-1_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-wapo-3-6-20-slater-1-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_of_Delhi" title="Environment of Delhi">Environment of Delhi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Delhi_aerial_photo_04-2016_img11.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Delhi_aerial_photo_04-2016_img11.jpg/220px-Delhi_aerial_photo_04-2016_img11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Delhi_aerial_photo_04-2016_img11.jpg/330px-Delhi_aerial_photo_04-2016_img11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Delhi_aerial_photo_04-2016_img11.jpg/440px-Delhi_aerial_photo_04-2016_img11.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Delhi_aerial_photo_04-2016_img11.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of Delhi in April 2016 with river <a href="/wiki/Yamuna" title="Yamuna">Yamuna</a> in top-right.</div></div></div>
<p>Delhi is located in <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">Northern India</a>, at <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1073938472">.mw-parser-output .geo-default,.mw-parser-output .geo-dms,.mw-parser-output .geo-dec{display:inline}.mw-parser-output .geo-nondefault,.mw-parser-output .geo-multi-punct{display:none}.mw-parser-output .longitude,.mw-parser-output .latitude{white-space:nowrap}</style><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Delhi&amp;params=28.61_N_77.23_E_"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">28°37′N</span> <span class="longitude">77°14′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">28.61°N 77.23°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">28.61; 77.23</span></span></span></a></span>. The city is bordered on its northern, western, and southern sides by the <a href="/wiki/States_and_union_territories_of_India" title="States and union territories of India">state</a> of <a href="/wiki/Haryana" title="Haryana">Haryana</a> and to the east by that of <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a> (UP). Two prominent features of the geography of Delhi are the Yamuna flood plains and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delhi_ridge" title="Delhi ridge">Delhi ridge</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yamuna_River" title="Yamuna River">Yamuna River</a> was the historical boundary between <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a> and UP, and its flood plains provide fertile alluvial soil suitable for agriculture but are prone to recurrent floods. The Yamuna, a sacred river in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, is the only major river flowing through Delhi. The <a href="/wiki/Hindon_River" title="Hindon River">Hindon River</a> separates Ghaziabad from the eastern part of Delhi. The Delhi ridge originates from the <a href="/wiki/Aravalli_Range" title="Aravalli Range">Aravalli Range</a> in the south and encircles the west, northeast, and northwest parts of the city. It reaches a height of 318 m (1,043 ft) and is a dominant feature of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gisridge_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-gisridge-73">[72]</a></sup> In addition to the wetlands formed by the Yamuna river, Delhi continues to retain over 500 ponds (wetlands &lt; 5 ha), that in turn support considerable number of bird species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-74">[73]</a></sup> Delhi's ponds, despite experiencing ecological deterioration due to garbage dumping and concretization, supports the largest number of bird species known to be using ponds anywhere in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-75">[74]</a></sup> Existing policy in Delhi prevents the conversion of wetlands and, quite inadvertently, has led to the city's ponds becoming invaluable refugia for birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The National Capital Territory of Delhi covers an area of 1,483 km<sup>2</sup> (573 sq mi), of which 783 km<sup>2</sup> (302 sq mi) is designated rural, and 700 km<sup>2</sup> (270 sq mi) urban therefore making it the largest city in terms of area in the country. It has a length of 51.9 km (32 mi) and a width of 48.48 km (30 mi).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Please also update List of cities in India by area when you add a citation (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Delhi is included in India's <a href="/wiki/Earthquake_zones_of_India" title="Earthquake zones of India">seismic zone-IV</a>, indicating its vulnerability to major earthquakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hazardprofile_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-hazardprofile-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Delhi" title="Climate of Delhi">Climate of Delhi</a></div>
<p>Delhi features a dry-winter <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">humid subtropical climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen</a> <i>Cwa</i>) bordering a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_semi-arid_climate" title="Hot semi-arid climate">hot semi-arid climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen</a> <i>BSh</i>). The warm season lasts from 21 March to 15 June with an average daily high temperature above 39 °C (102 °F). The hottest day of the year is 22 May, with an average high of 40 °C (104 °F) and low of 28 °C (82 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weatherspark_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-weatherspark-77">[76]</a></sup> The cold season lasts from 26 November to 9 February with an average daily high temperature below 20 °C (68 °F). The coldest day of the year is 4 January, with an average low of 2 °C (36 °F) and high of 14 °C (57 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weatherspark_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-weatherspark-77">[76]</a></sup> In early March, the wind direction changes from north-westerly to south-westerly. From April to October the weather is hot. The monsoon arrives at the end of June, along with an increase in humidity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-climate_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-climate-78">[77]</a></sup> The brief, mild winter starts in late November, peaks in January and heavy fog often occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fog_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fog-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Temperatures in Delhi usually range from 2 to 47 °C (35.6 to 116.6 °F), with the lowest and highest temperatures ever recorded being −2.2 and 49.2 °C (28.0 and 120.6 °F), respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> However, 49.2 °C (120.6 °F) was recorded at Mungeshpur on 15 May 2022 whereas one of the main weathering station, that is, Airport station recorded all time high of 48.4 °C (119.1 °F) on 26 May 1998. The lowest ever temperature ever recorded is −2.2 °C (28.0 °F) at airport on 11 January 1967. The annual mean temperature is 25 °C (77 °F); monthly mean temperatures range from 13 to 32 °C (55 to 90 °F). The highest temperature recorded in July was 45 °C (113 °F) in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weatherbase_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-weatherbase-82">[81]</a></sup> The average annual rainfall is approximately 886 mm (34.9 in), most of which falls during the monsoon in July and August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecosurv1_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-ecosurv1-21">[20]</a></sup> The average date of the advent of monsoon winds in Delhi is 29 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hindumonsoon_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-hindumonsoon-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

</td>
<td>8.9 kilometres per hour (5.5 mph)
</td></tr></tbody></table>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_pollution">Air pollution</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_in_Delhi" title="Environmental issues in Delhi">Environmental issues in Delhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution_in_Delhi" title="Air pollution in Delhi">Air pollution in Delhi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Poulluted_killer_fog_in_Delhi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Poulluted_killer_fog_in_Delhi.jpg/220px-Poulluted_killer_fog_in_Delhi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Poulluted_killer_fog_in_Delhi.jpg/330px-Poulluted_killer_fog_in_Delhi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Poulluted_killer_fog_in_Delhi.jpg/440px-Poulluted_killer_fog_in_Delhi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Poulluted_killer_fog_in_Delhi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A dense toxic smog in New Delhi blocks out the sun. In November 2017, Delhi's chief minister described the city as a "gas chamber".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) Delhi was the most polluted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> city in the world in 2014. In 2016 WHO downgraded Delhi to eleventh-worst in the urban air quality database.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> According to one estimate, air pollution causes the death of about 10,500 people in Delhi every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TimePollution_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-TimePollution-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VOAPollution_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-VOAPollution-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EconomistPollution_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-EconomistPollution-108">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Air_quality_index" title="Air quality index">Air quality index</a> of Delhi is generally moderate (101–200) level between January to September, and then it drastically deteriorates to Very Poor (301–400), Severe (401–500) or Hazardous (500+) levels in three months between October to December, due to various factors including stubble burning, fire crackers burning during Diwali and cold weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> During 2013–14, peak levels of fine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particulate_matter" title="Particulate matter">particulate matter</a> (PM) in Delhi increased by about 44%, primarily due to high vehicular and industrial emissions, construction work and crop burning in adjoining states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TimePollution_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-TimePollution-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJPollution_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJPollution-112">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GARDINER,_25_January_14_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-GARDINER,_25_January_14-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BEARAK,_8_feb_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-BEARAK,_8_feb-114">[113]</a></sup> It has the highest level of the airborne particulate matter, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PM2.5" title="PM2.5">PM2.5</a> considered most harmful to health, with 153 micrograms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rising air pollution level has significantly increased lung-related ailments (especially asthma and lung cancer) among Delhi's children and women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TodayPollution_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-TodayPollution-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNAPollution_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-DNAPollution-117">[116]</a></sup> The dense smog and haze in Delhi during winter results in major air and rail traffic disruptions every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReutersPollution_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReutersPollution-118">[117]</a></sup> According to Indian meteorologists, the average maximum temperature in Delhi during winters has declined notably since 1998 due to rising air pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>India's <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Earth_Sciences" title="Ministry of Earth Sciences">Ministry of Earth Sciences</a> published a research paper in October 2018 attributing almost 41% of PM2.5 air pollution in Delhi to vehicular emissions, 21.5% to dust/fire and 18% to industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoES5_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-MoES5-120">[119]</a></sup> The director of <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Science_and_Environment" title="Centre for Science and Environment">Centre for Science and Environment</a> (CSE) alleged that the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_in_India" title="Automotive industry in India">Society of Indian Automobile Manufacturers</a> (SIAM) is lobbying "against the report" because it is "inconvenient" to the automobile industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSE_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSE-121">[120]</a></sup> Environmentalists have also criticised the Delhi government for not doing enough to curb air pollution and to inform people about air quality issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VOAPollution_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-VOAPollution-107">[106]</a></sup> In 2014, an environmental panel appealed to India's Supreme Court to impose a 30% <a href="/wiki/Cess" title="Cess">cess</a> on diesel cars, but till date no action has been taken to penalise the automobile industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of Delhi's residents are unaware of alarming levels of air pollution in the city and the health risks associated with it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GARDINER,_25_January_14_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-GARDINER,_25_January_14-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BEARAK,_8_feb_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-BEARAK,_8_feb-114">[113]</a></sup> In 2020, annual average <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PM2.5" title="PM2.5">PM2.5</a> in the Delhi, stood at 107.6 µg/m³, which is almost 21.5 times the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> PM2.5 Guideline (5 µg/m³: set in September, 2021).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> These pollution levels are estimated to reduce the <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">Life Expectancy</a> of an average person living in Delhi by almost 10.1 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Delhi&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, awareness, particularly among the foreign diplomatic community and high-income Indians, was noticeably increasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT021415_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT021415-125">[124]</a></sup> Since the mid-1990s, Delhi has undertaken some measures to curb air pollution—Delhi has the third-highest quantity of trees among Indian cities<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Transport_Corporation" title="Delhi Transport Corporation">Delhi Transport Corporation</a> operates the world's largest fleet of environmentally friendly <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> (CNG) buses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cities.expressindia.com_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-cities.expressindia.com-127">[126]</a></sup> In 1996, the CSE started a public interest litigation in the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_India" title="Supreme Court of India">Supreme Court of India</a> that ordered the conversion of Delhi's fleet of buses and taxis to run on CNG and banned the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leaded_petrol" title="Leaded petrol">leaded petrol</a> in 1998. In 2003, Delhi won the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a>'s first 'Clean Cities International Partner of the Year' award for its "bold efforts to curb air pollution and support alternative fuel initiatives".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cities.expressindia.com_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-cities.expressindia.com-127">[126]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Metro" title="Delhi Metro">Delhi Metro</a> has also been credited for significantly reducing air pollutants in the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, according to several authors, most of these gains have been lost, especially due to <a href="/wiki/Stubble_burning" title="Stubble burning">stubble burning</a>, a rise in the market share of <a href="/wiki/Diesel_exhaust" title="Diesel exhaust">diesel cars</a> and a considerable decline in bus ridership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kumari_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-kumari-129">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSEPollution_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSEPollution-130">[129]</a></sup> According to CSE and System of Air Quality Weather Forecasting and Research (SAFAR), burning of agricultural waste in nearby Punjab, Haryana and Uttar Pradesh regions results in severe intensification of smog over Delhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ETTPollution_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-ETTPollution-131">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSE2Pollution_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSE2Pollution-132">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
